                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Aqueous+Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_aqueous%20sodium%20ion%20batteries">aqueous sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Iron+Phosphate&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20iron%20phosphate">sodium iron phosphate</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Layered+Structure&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_layered%20structure">layered structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Fast+Charging&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_fast%20charging">fast charging</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The pressing need for renewable and sustainable energy storage warrants the development of next-generation rechargeable batteries, which can feature low price, high safety, long cycling, and environmental friendliness simultaneously. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> State-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries have achieved tremendous success for mobile devices and electric vehicles; however, the scarce reserve and uneven distribution of Li elements would significantly hamper their large-scale applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a> In addition, the employment of volatile, flammable, and toxic aprotic electrolytes would incur safety and cost concerns. In this scenario, aqueous sodium-ion batteries (ASIBs) represent an attractive approach for stationary energy storage, due to the ubiquitous distribution of Na reserves and the intrinsic safety of aqueous electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The development of advanced electrode materials plays a pivotal role in ASIBs, which should integrate both excellent electrochemical performance and material sustainability. To date, a wide range of cathode materials, such as metal oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> phosphates, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a> and Prussian blue analogues, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 11 and 12">(11,12)</a> have demonstrated encouraging performance for sodium (Na) storage. On the contrary, the development of anode materials has remained stagnant. To our knowledge, among a few candidates, only NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> stands out as the most reasonable one. Operating on the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> redox in the sodium super ionic conductor (NASICON) structure, NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> exhibits a specific capacity of ∼100–110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, a low potential of −0.8 V (vs Ag/AgCl), and excellent cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> However, the hurdles of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> are also twofold. First, it exhibits an average Na-insertion potential of −0.8 V, accompanying with a widely used cutoff potential of −1.0 V, which are much lower than the theoretical potential of hydrogen evolution reactions (−0.61 V). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> This may trigger parasitic water decomposition during prolonged cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> Second, the expensive Ti elements and the delicate synthesis concurrently increase the material manufacturing cost. In addition, the indispensable carbon coating of NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> usually associates with the precursor pyrolysis in the inert atmosphere, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> which releases some volatile organic compounds or carbon monoxides that pertain to environmental pollutants. To mitigate these issues, it is highly desirable to develop an alternative anode that combines moderate potentials, material sustainability, and facile preparation simultaneously.</div><div class="NLM_p">Recently, iron-based phosphate materials have engendered increasing attention in Na-ion batteries, presumably due to their conspicuous merits of cost-effectiveness and low toxicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> More importantly, the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox couple usually exhibits a higher potential than the Ti<sup>4+</sup>/Ti<sup>3+</sup> one, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> which renders better compatibility of Fe-based compounds in aqueous electrolytes. In light of the literature, iron phosphates are reported to incorporate Na<sup>+</sup> ions at moderate potentials of 2.3–2.7 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in nonaqueous electrolytes, which corresponds to a favorable potential range of −0.6 to −0.2 V vs Ag/AgCl in aqueous electrolytes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17−19)</a> However, there have been few reports of these materials in aqueous systems, possibly due to the material dissolution and structure degradation. Zaghib et al. discovered that amorphous FePO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O could deliver a specific capacity of ∼70 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and an average reaction potential of −0.2 V, yet at the expense of insufficient cycling of 200 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Recently, our group demonstrated a NASICON-structured Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a feasible anode, which exhibits a moderate capacity of 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and cycling of 1000 cycles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Despite these efforts, it remains a grand challenge to develop a viable Fe-based material that rivals the prominent NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. As a matter of fact, sodium iron phosphates represent a large group of inorganic compounds with a general composition of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub><i>y</i></sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub><i>z</i></sub>, whose stoichiometry, crystal structures, and insertion potentials can be readily tuned. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 17 to 21">(17−21)</a> This brings tremendous opportunities for the rational design of Fe-based anode in aqueous batteries.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, we demonstrate a layered sodium iron phosphate of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> as a cost-effective and high-performance anode candidate in aqueous batteries. Its unique layered structure with ample spacings not only offers a favorable Na-diffusion channel, but also effectively alleviates the volume expansion during the two-Na-insertion reactions. Consequently, it exhibits a specific capacity of ∼83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, a desirable potential of −0.4 V, excellent rate of 200 C, and stable cycling of 6000 cycles. When coupled with a Prussian white cathode, the full cell provides a promising energy density of 46 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> for 3000 cycles. We also employed a set of advanced characterizatioasiun such as synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM), and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) to comprehensively elucidate its bulk structure and understand the resultant Na<sup>+</sup> insertion mechanism.</div></div><div id="sec50" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> materials were synthesized by a facile sol–gel method, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 22 and 23">(22,23)</a> which comprises inexpensive chemicals of NaNO<sub>3</sub>, Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>, and citrate acid as raw materials. Notably, the high-temperature annealing process takes place in air, which mitigates the production of volatile organic compounds or carbon monoxides. The as-prepared Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> material turns light yellow in color (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). To obtain the accurate structure information, we carried out synchrotron powder XRD and Rietveld refinement analysis. As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A, Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> exhibits sharp and strong XRD patterns with the presence of two undefined minor peaks (denoted as * in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>A), which indicates the high crystallinity. This material crystallizes in the monoclinic structure with a space group of C2/<i>c</i>, which agrees well with previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> We refined the lattice parameters as <i>a</i> = 19.73 Å, <i>b</i> = 6.42 Å, <i>c</i> = 10.64 Å, α = 90.0°, β = 91.8°, γ = 90.0° (<i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> = 8.62%) and listed other crystal information, such as the lattice position and occupancy of Na, Fe, P, and O atoms, in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/medium/mg1c00035_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural characterization of the layered Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> material. (A) Synchrotron XRD and the Rietveld refinement. Asterisk (*) denotes the undefined peaks from impurities. (B, C) Crystal structure observed from different directions. (D) FT-IR spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>B illustrates its bidimensional layered structure along the b direction. The FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra are alternately connected by PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra via either the corner or edge sharing, which builds up one complex framework layer. There exists another tier of Na<sup>+</sup> ions (yellow colors) that are sandwiched in-between these framework layers. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>C show its crystal structure in c direction for better visualization. The Na<sup>+</sup> ions that reside in the interslab layers are six coordinated to oxygen atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), which are denoted as Na1 sites. The rest Na<sup>+</sup> ions are seven coordinated to oxygen atoms (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> which was assigned as Na2 sites. Of note, both Na1 and Na2 ions are not extractable from the structure, because Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> exists in its oxidized state (Fe<sup>3+</sup>), which will accept electrons and accommodate extra Na<sup>+</sup> ions for charge balance. Such a layered structure is expected to benefit the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion kinetics and reversibility, as showcased by other layered phosphate materials such as Na<sub>2</sub>FePO<sub>4</sub>F and NaVOPO<sub>4</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">We employed Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) to analyze the bond information in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> material. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>D, the predominant overlapping of ν<sub>1</sub> and ν<sub>3</sub> in 1200∼900 cm<sup>–1</sup> arises from the stretching modes of (PO<sub>4</sub>)<sup>3–</sup> groups. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> The multiple peaks in 650∼500 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the deformation of O–P–O bonds owing to the ν<sub>2</sub> and ν<sub>4</sub> bending modes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> The peak between 650 and 710 cm<sup>–1</sup> is associated with the vibration of the Fe<sup>3+</sup>···O<sup>2–</sup> bonds in the FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedral structures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">We utilized scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and aberration-corrected scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) to further evaluate the morphology and structure of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>A, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> sample demonstrates a pellet-like morphology, with some flat flakes perpendicularly stacked to each other. This may result from its layered structure that preferentially grow along certain crystallographic direction. The particle size varies from 500 nm to 1 μm. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>B shows a representative transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image. The corresponding energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mapping demonstrates that Na, Fe, P, and O elements exhibit homogeneous distribution in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>C). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>D shows the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern. The bright diffraction spots in the Debye ring with an interplanar spacing of ∼0.62 and ∼0.54 nm can be attributed to the crystal plane spacing of (11̅0) and (11̅1), respectively. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>E shows the aberration-corrected STEM Z-contrast images of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> sample imaged along [110] zone axis. In such Z-contrast images, the contrast is proportional to the average atomic number of the atomic columns. Therefore, the high contrast spots in the Z-contrast images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>F show the position of Fe-containing atomic columns, which matches well with the monoclinic Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> crystal model.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/medium/mg1c00035_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Morphology and structure analysis of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. (A) A SEM image. (B, C) Representative HAADF-STEM image and the corresponding Na, Fe, P, and O elemental mapping. (D) Selected area electron diffraction pattern. (E, F) Atomic STEM images.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical characterization was conducted in three-electrode cells, which contain Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, activated carbon, and Ag/AgCl as the working electrode, counter electrode, and reference electrode, respectively. All the potentials in this paper are referred to Ag/AgCl unless noted otherwise. We chose to use the 17 mol kg<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub> “water-in-salt” (WiS) electrolyte, due to its enhanced electrochemical window and minimized side reactions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 to 34">(30−34)</a> which can bolster the Na-insertion performance in electrodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>A, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode demonstrates a reversible charge capacity of 83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1C rate (1C = 83 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), which corresponds to the insertion of 1.9 Na<sup>+</sup> ions per Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> formula. The first charge/discharge curve is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, which gives rise to a low Coulombic efficiency of ∼64%, possibly due to hydrogen evolution side reactions. The theoretical capacity based on the two-Na-insertion reaction is ∼87 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. This capacity is slightly lower than that of the reported NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> anode (∼100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> but surpasses that of any reported iron phosphate anode materials, such as FePO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O (∼70 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (∼60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> which represents a progress in developing Fe-based anode materials. This capacity is also comparable to some Ti-based anode materials, such as Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (∼79 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/medium/mg1c00035_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode. (A) GCD profiles at 1C rate. (B) Rate performance. (C) CV curves measured at various scan rates. (D) Analysis of the peak current and the scan rate. (E) Electrochemical impedance analysis. (F) Linear fit between Re(<i>Z</i>) and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> within the low-frequency region. (G) Cycling performance at 10C rate. (H) Comparison of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> with other reported phosphate anode materials in terms of reaction potentials and reversible capacities.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The average Na-insertion potential in Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> is −0.40 V due to the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> redox couple, which exceeds NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> by 0.3 V in the same electrolyte (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Such a higher potential will sacrifice the full cell energy density to some extent; however, it effectively enhances the electrode compatibility with aqueous electrolytes. We found that, in the self-discharge test (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> anode could retain 72% of its original capacity after 24 h storage, which is superior to 12% in NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. It is likely that the 0.3 V higher potential of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> minimizes the parasitic side reactions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 2 to 4">(2−4)</a> such as water decomposition, which gives rise to higher capacity retention. In this case, we deem that the improved compatibility and lower cost of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> may compensate its inferior capacity and voltage for battery application (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">The Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode exhibits a high rate performance. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>B, the charge capacity is 83, 77, 70, 65, 64, 60, and 55 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1, 2, 5, 10, 20, 30, 50, 80, and 100 C, respectively. Even at 200 C rate, Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> still retains a charge capacity of 42 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which indicates ∼50% capacity utilization in a short time of ∼9 s. Such rate performance is also better than some Fe or Ti-based anode materials, such as Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (100C, 62%), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (10C, 84%), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> and carbon-encapsulated NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (20C, 62%). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a> gives the corresponding GCD curves at various rates. Notably, the potential hysteresis between charge and discharge merely increases from 0.09 V at 1 C to 0.30 V at 200 C, which further corroborates the fast Na-insertion kinetics. We infer that it is the favorable layered structure with a large <i>d</i> spacing that facilitates the Na diffusion process. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To further understand the reaction kinetics, we measured the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves at different scanning rates (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>C). At 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> shows two pairs of broad oxidization/reduction peaks at −0.29/–0.31 V (O1/R1) and −0.33/–0.42 V (O2/R2), respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>), which likely stems from the two-Na-insertion mechanism. The reaction potentials also agree well with the two slops in GCD curves. With the increase of scan rates, the peak current proliferates accordingly. It has been suggested that the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the scanning rate (<i>v</i>) comply with a prominent equation of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, in which <i>b</i> is a varying constant that dictates the reaction mechanism. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Specifically, the reaction is diffusion-controlled when <i>b</i> equals 0.5, whereas the reaction is a capacitive one when <i>b</i> is unity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>D shows the linear fitting of log(<i>i</i>) and log(<i>v</i>), where the <i>b</i> values for the O1/R1 and O2/R2 couple are 0.75/0.74 and 0.83/0.89, respectively. This suggests that Na-insertion is a mixed-controlled reaction, with certain capacity coming from the capacitive contribution.</div><div class="NLM_p">We further tested the electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4)4</sub> electrode at a state-of-charge of 50%. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>E, the EIS results consist of a semicircle at the high-frequency region and a linear slope at the low-frequency region, which corresponds to the charge transfer impedance and the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion process, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The charge-transfer resistance is derived to be as low as 7.6 ohm, which suggests a fast-interfacial reaction. We also calculate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) based on the equation of <i>D</i> = <i>R</i><sup>2</sup><i>T</i><sup>2</sup>/2<i>A</i><sup>2</sup><i>n</i><sup>4</sup><i>F</i><sup>4</sup><i>C</i><sup>2</sup>σ<sup>2</sup>, where the parameters of <i>R</i>, <i>T</i>, <i>A</i>, <i>n</i>, <i>F</i>, and <i>C</i> denote the gas constant (8.314 J K<sup>–1</sup> mol<sup>–1</sup>), temperature (298 K), geometric area of electrodes (0.785 cm<sup>–2</sup>), electron transfer number (<i>n</i> = 2), faradaic constant (96,485 C mol<sup>–1</sup>), and Na<sup>+</sup> concentration (1.01 × 10<sup>–3</sup> mol cm<sup>–3</sup>), respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> The Warburg factor σ would derive from the linear fit between the real impedance (Re) and reciprocal square root of the angular frequency (ω<sup>–1/2</sup>), which is determined as 7.82 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>F).</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on these values, we estimate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient in the layered Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> structure as 4.8 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, which is on par with other phosphate materials such as Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (4.7 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (10<sup>–9</sup>–10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a> Besides its reasonable capacity and rate capability, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode also exhibits an outstanding cycling performance. When tested at low rate of 1 C, the charge capacity only fades from 83 to 61 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> over 260 cycles, which corresponds to a capacity retention of 75% with an average Coulombic efficiency of ∼97.2%. (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). When cycled at 10 C rate, the electrode experiences noticeable capacity fading in the initial 400 cycles but maintains very stable cycling in the following 5600 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>G). The average Coulombic efficiency is ∼99.93%. Considering the low volume change of electrodes, we infer that the initial capacity loss may result from the slight material dissolution, due to the color change of the separator (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). Overall, Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> demonstrates a high capacity retention of ∼72% over 6000 cycles, which is much superior to other Fe-based phosphates such as FePO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O (200 cycles) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> and Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (1000 cycles), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and comparable to some Ti-based materials such as NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> anode <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref13 ref14 ref37 ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref13 ref14 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 4, 13, 14, 37 and 38">(4,13,14,37,38)</a> and Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3/2</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> shows the GCD curves at different cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">Aqueous batteries are usually cycled at higher current rates, which can mask some side reactions and thus strategically increase the cycling numbers. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2" aria-label="reference 2">(2)</a> To further examine the cycling stability of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode, we also tested the cycling at low rates of 0.2C and 1C with practically high mass loading of 20 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> shows 96% capacity retention after 150 cycles (0.2C rate) and 86% retention after 500 cycles (1C rate), which further corroborates its cycling stability. The charge/discharge curve at 0.1C rate is given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>. On the other hand, one may be concerned about the use of concentrated NaClO<sub>4</sub> electrolytes, which may increase the full cell cost. Therefore, we also tested its performance in diluted 1 M Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> electrolyte. As shown, it exhibits a similar capacity of 83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and reasonable retention of 74% after 3000 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>), which indicates its applicability in conventional diluted electrolytes. Meanwhile, among highly soluble sodium salts, NaClO<sub>4</sub> has a much lower price than those of the commonly used fluorinated salts, such as NaFSI, NaTFSI, and NaOTF (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>), which suggests the salt affordability for aqueous battery application.</div><div class="NLM_p">To have a better picture of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> in the context of phosphate anode materials, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>H shows its comparison with reported materials of FePO<sub>4</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> NaV<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>MgTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> Na<sub>2</sub>VTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> and NaTi<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> in terms of capacities and reaction potentials. The rate and cycling performance comparison are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>. Evidently, Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> affords a comprehensively attractive performance, such as a reasonable capacity, a suitable potential (slightly higher than HER), a high-rate capability, and long-term cycling stability. More importantly, it comprises abundant and nontoxic Na, Fe, and P elements only, which would be a conspicuous advantage over Ti- or V-based phosphate compounds for large-scale applications.</div><div class="NLM_p">To unravel the Na-insertion mechanism in the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> anode, we first employed operando synchrotron XRD to investigate its crystal structural evolution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 43 to 46">(43−46)</a> The cell was tested at a moderate rate of 0.5 C. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>A and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S15</a>, there is no emergence of new peaks or absence of original peaks during the entire charge/discharge process, and each XRD pattern can be assigned to the monoclinic structure. This implies that Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> does not undergo a phase transition, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> which agrees with its S-shaped GCD slops. Careful observation reveals that (200), (110), and (022) peaks experience noticeable shifts to lower angles during discharge, which can be rationalized by the lattice expansion due to Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Upon charging, with Na<sup>+</sup> ions extracting from the lattice, these XRD peaks are restored to their original positions, which corresponds to a reversible structure evolution process.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/medium/mg1c00035_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Investigation of Na-insertion mechanism in Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>. (A) Contour plot of the operando synchrotron XRD patterns. (B) Variation of the lattice parameters (<i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i>) during the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion process. (C) Evolution of the unit cell volume during the Na<sup>+</sup> (de)insertion process. (D) 2D contour plot of the in situ XANES spectra. (E) Fe K-edge spectra during discharge. (F) Fe K-edge spectra upon charging.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Based on Bragg’s law, we quantitatively calculated the detail change of the lattice parameters (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>B), where <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> change from 19.7, 6.4, and 10.7 Å in the pristine state to 20.0, 6.5, and 10.7 Å in the discharged state, respectively. Apparently, the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion predominantly expands the structure along the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> direction (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>), whereas the complex framework layer (along the <i>c</i> direction) remains intact. We further discover that the overall volume of the unit cell merely increases only from∼1340 to 1385 Å<sup>3</sup>, which corresponds to a low volume expansion of ∼3.4% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>C). Such a value is smaller than that of most phosphate anode materials (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>), and it is even smaller than that for Li<sup>+</sup> insertion in graphite (10.3%). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47 ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47 ref48" aria-label="reference 47 and 48">(47,48)</a> We infer that it is the favorable layered structure with large interlayer spacings that relieves the Na<sup>+</sup>-insertion strain, which translates to the impressive cycling stability of 6000 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">To obtain the Fe oxidation state information during battery charge–discharge, we carried out in situ X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) measurements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>D–F, during discharge, there is a continuous shift of the Fe K-edge spectra from a higher (7124.4 eV) to lower (7121.2 eV) energy state, which is indicative of the reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions due to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. Upon charge, the Fe K-edge spectra gradually return to the original states as the pristine material, which, again, demonstrate the reaction reversibility. We further analyzed the Fe oxidation by fitting the K-edge spectra in reference to the standard FeO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> compounds (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> We find that Fe ions have an oxidation state of +3 in the pristine material, which decreases to an average state of +2.3 in the fully discharged material. This clearly supports the two-electron reaction for the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> anode, which converts to Na<sub>5</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> at the end of the discharge, with a theoretical Fe oxidation state of +2.33.</div><div class="NLM_p">We also observed the subtle local structure changes from the extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) as a result of the Fe oxidation state change (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>). On the basis of model-based fitting, we found that the pristine Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> material has a Fe–O bond length of 1.96 Å. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> With the continuous reduction of Fe<sup>3+</sup> during discharge, the Fe–O bonds start to increase due to the decreasing charge density on the Fe absorbers. The fully discharged state gives rise to a Fe–O bond of 2.02 Å. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Upon charging, the Fe–O bond length decreases to a length of 1.99 Å, which suggests the reversible change in local structures. These small bond changes are expected to show negligible influence on the anode structure, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> which also accounts for the long-term cycling stability. To our knowledge, this is also the first report of a layered phosphate material that supports reversible Na-insertion in aqueous electrolytes.</div><div class="NLM_p">To emphasize the concept of iron-based materials for aqueous battery operation, we selected a Fe-based Prussian white (PW) of Na<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> as the cathode material, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> which can be readily prepared from inexpensive raw materials via aqueous precipitation reactions (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19</a>). This cathode offers a reasonable capacity of 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, an exceedingly high potential of +0.8 V, and a stable cycling performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20</a>). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>A schematically shows the working principle of the Fe-based ASIB, where both the cathode and anode materials utilize the identical Fe<sup>III</sup>/Fe<sup>II</sup> couples for redox reactions. The full-cell reaction is expressed as Na<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sup>III</sup><sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> ↔ Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> + Na<sub>5</sub>Fe<sup>II</sup><sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub>, which yields a meaningful cell voltage due to the difference of Fe coordination environments and crystal structures. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/medium/mg1c00035_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of the aqueous Na-ion full cell. (A) Scheme of the ASIB based on the Na<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> cathode and the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> anode. (B) GCD profiles at 1C rate. (C) Rate performance. (D) Abundance of transition metal elements in the Earth’s crust. (E) Prices of transition metal elements based on the bulk trade of commodities. (F) Comparison of our work with some representative aqueous Na-ion batteries or capacitors in aspects of average capacity, working voltage, and energy density.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/asset/images/large/mg1c00035_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The full cell exhibits an approximate N/P ratio of 1:1 (N, negative; P, positive). As displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>B, it exhibits a specific capacity of 80 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> during discharge (based on the anode mass) and an average operation voltage of ∼1.2 V, which corresponds to a respectable energy density of ∼46 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (based on the total mass of cathode and anode). The majority of the capacity is above 0.8 V, which assures a meaningful output voltage. The full cell also affords an excellent rate capability, which retains ∼55% capacity utilization at 200 C rate (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>C). The Ragone plot is provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S21</a>. It is likely that the open framework of the PW cathode and the layered structure of the phosphate anode benefits the fast-charging property. Additionally, the full cell shows low overpotentials at various rate, which are conducive to the round-trip energy efficiency. From 1 to 100 C rate, the energy efficiency is higher than 80% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S22</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S23</a> shows the cycling performance of the full cell, which exhibits high capacity retention of ∼72% after 3000 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Albeit the substantial progress of aqueous Na-ion full cells in recent years, most of them are at the expense of using scarce or expensive transition elements, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39 ref40 ref41 ref42 ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 39 to 42 and 52 to 55">(39−42,52−55)</a> such as Co, V, Ni, Cu, and Ti, which will compromise the benefits of aqueous batteries. Thus, we would like to underline the importance of elemental abundance and price from the material perspective. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>D and E shows the Earth abundance and average prices of these elements, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56 ref57" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 56 and 57">(56,57)</a> Apparently, V, Co, Ni, and Cu elements exhibit low abundance in the Earth and thus incur high prices. Moreover, V and Co elements are usually toxic, which further hampers their large-scale utilization. Despite the acceptable abundance of Ti elements, their relatively high price ($11 USD/kg) may substantially increase the electrode manufacturing cost. Therefore, it is more desirable to assemble aqueous batteries that comprise inexpensive Fe, Mn, or Zn elements. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> To have a better picture of the ASIB studies, we summarized the elemental compositions and cell performance of representative Na-ion full cells in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>F. As shown, our work utilizes the Earth-abundant Fe element primarily and provides encouraging cell performance, which represents a step forward for developing Fe-based aqueous batteries.</div></div><div id="sec100" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we revealed fast and durable Na<sup>+</sup> insertion properties for a layered sodium iron phosphate Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> and applied it as an appealing anode candidate for aqueous Na-ion batteries. Based on the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> couple, this electrode exhibits an outstanding two-Na-insertion performance, which gives rise to a good capacity of 83 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a desirable reaction potential of −0.4 V. Furthermore, an advanced characterization method (operando synchrotron XRD) demonstrated that its unique layered structure with a large <i>d</i>-spacing is indispensable to alleviate the volume expansion (∼3.4%) during the Na-insertion, which translates to impressive cycling stability. In addition, the potentially low cost and easy preparation makes it attractive for large-scale energy storage. Our work here exemplifies the importance of rational structural design in building low-cost and high-performance Fe-based materials for battery operation, and we envision a myriad of Fe-based polyanionic compounds awaiting intensive exploration in aqueous electrolytes.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> sample was synthesized by an easy sol–gel method using citric acid as the complexing agent. First, stoichiometric quantities of Fe(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>3</sub>·9H<sub>2</sub>O (3 mmol, Sigma-Aldrich, ≥99.95% trace metals basis), NaNO<sub>3</sub> (3 mmol, Sigma-Aldrich, ACS reagent, ≥99.0%), and NH<sub>4</sub>H<sub>2</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> (4 mmol, Sigma-Aldrich, ≥99.99% trace metals basis) were dissolved in 15 mL of deionized water. Then, excessive citric acid (15 mmol, Sigma-Aldrich, ACS reagent, ≥99.5%) was added dropwise into the mixed solution under stirring on a hot plate (60 °C). The stoichiometry of citric acid is 50% more than the total amount of cations (Fe<sup>3+</sup>, Na<sup>+</sup>, and NH<sub>4</sub><sup>+</sup>). The solution kept stirring until the solvent largely evaporated, which turned into a green gel. We then transferred the gel into an oven at 60 °C for overnight. The precursor was grounded into fine powders, which were sintered in the muffle oven at 750 °C for 3 days. The final powder products appeared light-yellow (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsmaterialsau.1c00035/suppl_file/mg1c00035_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1, Supporting Information</a>). The preparation of Na<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> was based on a simple aqueous precipitation method. We added the ZnSO<sub>4</sub> solution (40 mL 0.06 mol L<sup>–1</sup>) drop by drop into Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> solution (40 mL 0.09 mol L<sup>–1</sup>) under magnetic stirring. Once these two solutions were mixed, white precipitation took place immediately. The Na<sub>3</sub>Zn<sub>2</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub> sample was rinsed with deionized water several times and then transferred to an oven at 60 °C overnight.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The crystal structure identification and the Rietveld refinement of lattice parameters of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> sample were carried out by synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD). Powder XRD patterns were collected at the experimental station 13-BM of Advanced Photon Source (APS) of Argonne National Lab (ANL). The XAS data was analyzed with Athena to subtract the pre-edge background by linearly fitting and postedge background by using a cubic-spline fit procedure. The background subtracted XAS data was normalized by the height of absorption edge. The normalized data can be separated into two parts for analysis: XANES (oxidization state) and EXAFS (local structure). For quantitative analysis of EXAFS, Artemis software was used for the analysis. The scattering path of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> was generated by running the FEFF calculations based on the crystal structures of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> which is generated from XRD refinement. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the pristine electrode material Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> were taken at the OSU Electron Microscopy Facility using a field emission SEM (FEI Helios 650) at 15 kV. STEM was performed using a Cs- corrected FEI Themis Z G2.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Tests</h3><div class="NLM_p">To improve the electric conductivity, the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> material was ball-milled with Ketjen black carbon in a mass ratio of 8:1 for 4 h. The working electrode was composed of 80 wt % active materials, i.e., Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> or Na<sub>2</sub>Zn<sub>3</sub>[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>2</sub>, 10 wt % Ketjen black carbon, and 10 wt % poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) binder. The slurry was then coated on the carbon fiber papers, which were dried in an oven at 60 °C. The typical mass loading of working electrodes is 2.0–4.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The counter electrode was a piece of self-standing active carbon film, which consists of 70 wt % activated carbon, 20 wt % super P carbon, and 10 wt % polyterafluorothhylene (PTFE) binder. The thick Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrode is a self-standing film with active mass loading of ∼20 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. We examined the electrochemical performance of Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> in three-electrode Swagelok cells, which contain active materials as the working electrode, active carbon as the counter electrode, and Ag/AgCl (3.0 M KCl, E = +0.21 V vs standard hydrogen electrode, SHE) as the reference electrode. The electrolyte is a WiS electrolyte of 17 mol kg<sup>–1</sup> NaClO<sub>4</sub>. The Na<sup>+</sup>/Na couple has a redox potential of −2.7 V vs SHE (standard hydrogen electrode), whereas the Ag/AgCl reference electrode is approximately 0.2 V vs SHE. If a Na-ion electrode can host Na<sup>+</sup> ions at a potential of <i>m</i> V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na, it corresponds to (<i>m</i> – 2.7) vs SHE, which further translates to (<i>m</i> – 2.9) vs Ag/AgCl. Therefore, the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion potential range of 2.3–2.7 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na in iron phosphate compounds can translate to −0.6 to −0.2 V vs Ag/AgCl in aqueous electrolytes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were carried out on a CHI760E potentiostat. The galvanostatic charge/discharge curves were tested on a Landt battery tester (CT2001A) at room temperature. The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>4</sub> electrodes were tested on a CHI760E potentiostat, where the frequency was from 1 MHz to 0.1 Hz with an oscillation amplitude of 10 mV.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes2"><div class="article_content-header"><div         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Building a low-carbon society supported by sustainable energy is a worldwide topic.  The aq. lithium-ion battery (LIB) has been demonstrated to be one of the most promising stationary power sources for sustainable energies such as wind and solar power.  The aq. LIB may solve both the safety problem assocd. with the lithium-ion batteries which use highly toxic and flammable org. solvents, and the poor cycling life assocd. with commercialized aq. rechargeable batteries including lead-acid and nickel-metal-hydride systems.  During the past decades, many efforts have been made to improve the performance of the aq. lithium-ion battery.  The present work reviews the latest advances in the exploration and development of battery systems and relative materials.  Also the main approaches, achievements, and challenges in this field are briefly commented on and discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201800561"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">30 Years of Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. The battery behaviors and capacitive properties of this electrode system are critically investigated by using 1 M Li2SO4, Na2SO4, and K2SO4 electrolytes, with an optimal performance found to arise in Na+-based electrolyte, which exhibits a capacitance of 209 Fg-1 at 8.5 C as well as enhanced ion diffusion. In the case of K+-based electrolyte, NVP presents asym. cyclic voltammograms, owing to weak solvation and the high cond. of K+, making the ions more easily able to form elec. double-layer capacitance on the surface or pores of NVP, rather than insert into the network.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Low-defect Prussian blue nanocubes as high capacity and long life cathodes for aqueous Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Luo, Yang; Sun, Mengying; Qian, Jiangfeng; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>

                        cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Surfactant widens the electrochemical window of an aqueous electrolyte for better rechargeable aqueous sodium/zinc battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hou, Zhiguo; Zhang, Xueqian; Li, Xiaona; Zhu, Yongchun; Liang, Jianwen; Qian, Yitai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Aq. rechargeable batteries have received significant attention because of their low-cost and security.  However, the narrow electrochem. stability window (about 1.23 V) of the aq. electrolyte sets a limit on their energy output.  Herein, we have developed an aq. rechargeable hybrid battery using Na2MnFe(CN)6 nanocubes as the cathode and a zinc metal sheet as the anode, which delivered a high energy d. of 170 W h kg-1 and a capacity retention of 75% over 2000 cycles with an operating voltage of up to 2.0 V.  By adding sodium dodecyl sulfate (SDS) to the aq. electrolyte, the electrochem. stability window of the electrolyte was expanded to about 2.5 V.  The results of the expts. and calcns. based on the d. functional theory indicate that SDS can not only inhibit the decompn. of water, suppress the dissoln. of Mn and the corrosion of zinc but also increase the cycle life and rate capability.  The low-cost, high energy d., and long cycle life of the battery suggest that it is a promising candidate for energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1149/1.3611434"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Park, S.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Stable Iron- and Manganese-Based Cathodes for Long-Lasting Sodium Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Hyungsub; Yoon, Gabin; Park, Inchul; Hong, Jihyun; Park, Kyu-Young; Kim, Jongsoon; Lee, Kug-Seung; Sung, Nark-Eon; Lee, Seongsu; Kang, Kisuk</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of long-lasting and low-cost rechargeable batteries lies at the heart of the success of large-scale energy storage systems for various applications.  We introduce Fe- and Mn-based Na rechargeable battery cathodes that can stably cycle &gt;3000 times.  The new cathode is based on the solid-soln. phases of Na4MnxFe3-x(PO4)2(P2O7) (x =1 or 2) that we synthesized for the 1st time.  Electrochem. anal. and ex-situ structural investigation reveal that the electrodes operate via a 1-phase reaction upon charging and discharging with a remarkably low vol. change of 2.1% for Na4MnFe2(PO4)(P2O7), which is one of the lowest values among Na battery cathodes reported thus far.  With merits including an open framework structure and a small vol. change, a stable cycle performance ≤3000 cycles can be achieved at 1C and room temp., and ∼70% of the capacity at C/20 can be obtained at 20C.  We believe that these materials are strong competitors for large-scale Na-ion battery cathodes based on their low costs, long-term cycle stability, and high energy d.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1021/acs.jpcc.7b09803"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fernández-Ropero, A.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Toward Safe and Sustainable Batteries: Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 as a Low-Cost Cathode for Rechargeable Aqueous Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fernandez-Ropero, Antonio J.; Zarrabeitia, Maider; Reynaud, Marine; Rojo, Teofilo; Casas-Cabanas, Montse</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">122</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">133-142</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JPCCCK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1932-7447</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. properties of Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 in aq. and org. electrolyte are compared under similar conditions.  Na4Fe3(PO4)2P2O7 is able to deliver almost the same capacity in both types of electrolytes despite the smaller electrochem. window entailed by the aq. one.  As shown by electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS), this is possible thanks to the lower overpotential that this material exhibits in aq. electrolyte.  It is shown here that the main contribution to overpotential in org. electrolyte mainly originates from a SPI (Solid Permeable Interphase) layer formed below 3.5 V vs Na+/Na that acts as a blocking layer and hinders Na+ diffusion and that is absent in aq. electrolyte.  Overall, the obtained results highlight the pos. attributes of using low-cost and environmentally friendly aq. electrolytes and the challenges to be overcome in terms of air and moisture stability of the studied material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-09170-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cortie, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Indris, S.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NASICON-type air-stable and all-climate cathode for sodium-ion batteries with low cost and high-power density</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit18R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">18</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Decoupling of mechanical properties and ionic conductivity in supramolecular lithium ion conductors</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Mackanic, David G.; Yan, Xuzhou; Zhang, Qiuhong; Matsuhisa, Naoji; Yu, Zhiao; Jiang, Yuanwen; Manika, Tuheen; Lopez, Jeffrey; Yan, Hongping; Liu, Kai; Chen, Xiaodong; Cui, Yi; Bao, Zhenan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The emergence of wearable electronics puts batteries closer to the human skin, exacerbating the need for battery materials that are robust, highly ionically conductive, and stretchable.  Herein, we introduce a supramol. design as an effective strategy to overcome the canonical tradeoff between mech. robustness and ionic cond. in polymer electrolytes.  The supramol. lithium ion conductor utilizes orthogonally functional H-bonding domains and ion-conducting domains to create a polymer electrolyte with unprecedented toughness (29.3 MJ m-3) and high ionic cond. (1.2 × 10-4 S cm-1 at 25°C).  Implementation of the supramol. ion conductor as a binder material allows for the creation of stretchable lithium-ion battery electrodes with strain capability of over 900% via a conventional slurry process.  The supramol. nature of these battery components enables intimate bonding at the electrode-electrolyte interface.  Combination of these stretchable components leads to a stretchable battery with a capacity of 1.1 mAh cm-2 that functions even when stretched to 70% strain.  The method reported here of decoupling ionic cond. from mech. properties opens a promising route to create high-toughness ion transport materials for energy storage applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1016/j.ensm.2019.02.017"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Na<sub>4</sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>P<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub>/C nanospheres as low-cost, high-performance cathode material for sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Energy Storage Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>22</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">330</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">336</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ultra-low cost and highly stable hydrated FePO4 anodes for aqueous sodium-ion battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yuesheng; Feng, Zimin; Laul, Dharminder; Zhu, Wen; Provencher, Manon; Trudeau, Michel L.; Guerfi, Abdelbast; Zaghib, Karim</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">374</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing demands for large-scale energy storage devices have put a spotlight on aq. sodium-ion batteries, which possess a no. of highly desirable features, such as sodium abundance, low cost and safety over org. electrolytes.  While lots of cathode materials were reported, only few candidate materials like active carbon and NaTi2(PO4)3 were proposed as anodes.  It is a long-standing common knowledge that the low cost, non-toxicity, and highly reversible FePO4·2H2O is known as an attractive cathode material for non-aq. lithium- and sodium-ion batteries, but we demonstrate for the first time that nano-size non-carbon coated amorphous FePO4·2H2O can be used as the anode for an aq. sodium-ion battery.  Its optimum operating voltage (∼2.75 V vs. Na+/Na) avoids hydrogen evolution.  The capacity is as high as 80 mAh/g at a rate of 0.5 C in a three-electrode system.  The full cell, using the Na0.44MnO2 as cathode, maintained 90% of the capacity at 300 cycles at a rate of 3 C.  The calcns. also show that its vol. change during the intercalation of Na ions is below 2%.  Its low cost, high safety, along with its outstanding electrochem. performance makes amorphous FePO4·2H2O a promising anode material for aq. sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2019.103941"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lucero, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, M.</span></span><span class="NLM_etal"></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NASICON-type Na<sub>3</sub>Fe<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> as a low-cost and high-rate anode material for aqueous sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>64</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">103941</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-type Na3Fe2(PO4)3 as a low-cost and high-rate anode material for aqueous sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Qiu, Shen; Wu, Xianyong; Wang, Maoyu; Lucero, Marcos; Wang, Yan; Wang, Jie; Yang, Zhenzhen; Xu, Wenqian; Wang, Qi; Gu, Meng; Wen, Jianguo; Huang, Yaqin; Xu, Zhichuan J.; Feng, Zhenxing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
